    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer17178162843762329963">Ranked among the classic novels of the English language and the inspiration for several unforgettable movies, this early work of H. G. Wells was greeted in 1896 by howls of protest from reviewers, who found it horrifying and blasphemous. They wanted to know more about the wondrous possibilities of science shown in his first book, <i>The Time Machine</i>, not its potential for mis</span>
  <span id="freeText17178162843762329963" style="display:none">Ranked among the classic novels of the English language and the inspiration for several unforgettable movies, this early work of H. G. Wells was greeted in 1896 by howls of protest from reviewers, who found it horrifying and blasphemous. They wanted to know more about the wondrous possibilities of science shown in his first book, <i>The Time Machine</i>, not its potential for misuse and terror. In <i>The Island of Dr. Moreau</i>, a shipwrecked gentleman named Edward Prendick, stranded on a Pacific island lorded over by the notorious Dr. Moreau, confronts dark secrets, strange creatures, and a reason to run for his life.<br /><br />While this riveting tale was intended to be a commentary on evolution, divine creation, and the tension between human nature and culture, modern readers familiar with genetic engineering will marvel at Wells’s prediction of the ethical issues raised by producing “smarter” human beings or bringing back extinct species. These levels of interpretation add a richness to Prendick’s adventures on Dr. Moreau’s island of lost souls without distracting from what is still a rip-roaring good read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17178162843762329963" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/204948-is-this-book-too-gory-for-a-young-adult">Is this book too gory for a Young Adult Book Club?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_204948'><a id="like_count_community_question_204948" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/204948?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F29981.The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/204948-is-this-book-too-gory-for-a-young-adult">6 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/204948-is-this-book-too-gory-for-a-young-adult">See all 6 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/21579079-anna"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1536926030i/21579079._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/21579079-anna">Anna</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Definitely not. The goriest bits are blood, mentions of "open wounds," sever animal cruelty, and the vivisection, which is never described in full, on<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Definitely not. The goriest bits are blood, mentions of "open wounds," sever animal cruelty, and the vivisection, which is never described in full, only alluded to. <br /><br />If you read this book for a book club, you might also be interested in the short story The Most Dangerous Game by Richard Connell. The short story is very similar to this book and very thrilling with little to no gore involved. You could compare the two stories and the two antagonists quite well, drawing parallels between the main themes of each story and how they related to the time period they were written for. (The Island of Dr. Mareau was originally written as social commentary on the practice of vivisection.)<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/464594-hello-community-it-has-been-suggested-to">Hello community! It has been suggested to me that I should read the introduction of this book before actually reading the book - there are spoilers though. Does anyone advise I read the intro before or after the story itself? Thanks for the help!</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_464594'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F29981.The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/464594-hello-community-it-has-been-suggested-to">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/464594-hello-community-it-has-been-suggested-to">See all 3 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/39346323-greg"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1513962691i/39346323._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/39346323-greg">Greg</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Depends on the edition. If it's the author's introduction, absolutely. If it is someone else's introduction, then that someone might tell you too much<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Depends on the edition. If it's the author's introduction, absolutely. If it is someone else's introduction, then that someone might tell you too much about the story. I make it a point never to read introductions unless they were originally written by the author upon publication of the book. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_884').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>

<div id="review_1698891758" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1698891758" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1698891758?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141389394" href="/book/show/15990644-the-island-of-doctor-moreau">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1698891758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16471282210096586738">H.G. Wells is undoubtedly an exceptional human being!<br/><br/>Apart from the fact that "The Island of Doctor Moreau" is clearly part of the Victorian science fiction tradition, it contains all elements of a timeless study of the human condition, as well as a reflection on issues that are more worrying now than they were in the 19th century.<br/><br/>Do scientists have to follow ethical rules, or are they entitled to indulge in experiments that satisfy their curiosity, regardless of the consequences? In the traditi</span>
  <span id="freeText16471282210096586738" style="display:none">H.G. Wells is undoubtedly an exceptional human being!<br /><br />Apart from the fact that "The Island of Doctor Moreau" is clearly part of the Victorian science fiction tradition, it contains all elements of a timeless study of the human condition, as well as a reflection on issues that are more worrying now than they were in the 19th century.<br /><br />Do scientists have to follow ethical rules, or are they entitled to indulge in experiments that satisfy their curiosity, regardless of the consequences? In the tradition of a kind of pre-catastrophe <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">Frankenstein</a>, Doctor Moreau himself answers the question without any doubt:<br /><br />"I asked a question, devised some method of getting an answer, and got - a fresh question. Was this possible, or that possible? You cannot imagine what this means to an investigator, what an intellectual passion grows upon him. [...] To this day, I have never troubled about the ethics of the matter."<br /><br />While Wells leaves it to Moreau's creatures to punish him for this detached attitude, I am reminded of a real scientist who reflected upon the question himself, and understood the ethical dilemma of unrestrained science. When Oppenheimer quoted the "Bhagadvad Gita" to express his pain over his contribution to the development of the atomic bomb, he illustrated the path towards responsible science: "I have become Death, the destroyer of worlds!" <br /><br />The need to understand the consequences of scientific curiosity is the implicit message. Only if we manage to act responsibly with our inventions, hope in the future will be possible. Interestingly, Wells ends his story with the notion of hope, not because there is any reason for it, but because it is not possible to live without it. This closes the circle of Pandora's box, opened out of curiosity, unleashing all the terrors of the world, but leaving hope for humankind to be able to bear its fate.<br /><br />Apart from the obvious question of science and ethics, I found another story line in the short novel equally interesting. <br /><br />What makes us human? <br /><br />Main character Prendick paraphrases Descartes' idea when he notes: "He was a human then[...] for he could talk." <br /><br />Being able to communicate thoughts, feelings and ideas certainly makes us human, and it makes us storytelling animals, readers, Goodreads users. Over and over again, we repeat our stories, we reread them and re-interpret them, and I find it almost heart-breaking to follow the Beast Men's ritualistic repetition of the story they commit to - The Law, told with authority, transmitted as a poem to recite. It evokes the development of Margaret Atwood's Crakers, who also need religious origin stories and powerful poetical words to become fully human. Her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262203.MaddAddam__MaddAddam___3_" title="MaddAddam (MaddAddam, #3) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">MaddAddam</a> develops the idea of humanity as a community based on mythical storytelling to perfection, but Wells reflected on the same theme, as did Oppenheimer, when he chose to quote a timeless Indian classic to express his feelings of distress regarding the creation of modern horror.<br /><br />Looking around my house on this typical Swedish sunless summer day, I can only agree with the definition of humanity as a bunch of voracious story consumers:<br /><br />My eldest son is on the living room sofa, reading Zola's "Germinal", and I am vaguely jealous that he gets to do it for the FIRST time. The magic of it! My middle child is on his bed, reading a fabulous golden hardback version of Star Wars, the trilogy, and the story behind this reading adventure is well worth reflecting on: he found it in a bookstore, and begged me to buy it, despite the fact that he had already seen the movies, and we have as a rule that you read first and watch then. But since he DIDN'T KNOW there was a (thick!) novel, he asked for permission to reverse the procedure. Verdict on his part: so much more detail in the book! My youngest child is at the kitchen table with a pile of books that she seems to be reading simultaneously: She is in the middle of the Laura Ingalls Wilder series, and well into the fourth or fifth of "Anne of Green Gables". Oh, to be going back to Avonlea with her. Another memory of childhood reading bliss! <br /><br />So, I can hear my Middle School students pointing out that I am digressing from the digression right now, but my point is that "The Island of Doctor Moreau" brought it back to me why I read in the first place, why it makes me feel happy even when the content of the book scares and worries me. There is something unifying, peaceful and fulfilling in sharing books over cultural, generational and language borders, and it gives me hope for the future, even in times of violence.<br /><br />I will let Prendick have the last words, since he inspired this digression:<br /><br />"There it must be, I think, in the vast and eternal laws of matter, and not in the daily cares and sins and troubles of men, that whatever is more than animal within us must find its solace and its hope. I hope, or I could not live."</span>
  <a data-text-id="16471282210096586738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193584279?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193584279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4306085266166017674">"Not to go on all-Fours; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br/><br/>Not to suck up Drink; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br/><br/>Not to eat Flesh or Fish; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br/><br/>Not to claw the Bark of Trees; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br/><br/>Not to chase other Men; that is the Law. Are we not Men?"<br/><br/>H.G. Wells 1896 novella The Island of Dr. Moreau may have been a science fiction / fantasy precursor of William Golding’s 1954 classic <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies' title='Lord of the Flies by William Golding' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lord of the Flies</a>. Both works explore the theme of the fragility of humanity</span>
  <span id="freeText4306085266166017674" style="display:none">"Not to go on all-Fours; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br><br>Not to suck up Drink; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br><br>Not to eat Flesh or Fish; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br><br>Not to claw the Bark of Trees; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br><br>Not to chase other Men; that is the Law. Are we not Men?"<br><br>H.G. Wells 1896 novella The Island of Dr. Moreau may have been a science fiction / fantasy precursor of William Golding’s 1954 classic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Lord of the Flies by William Golding" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord of the Flies</a>. Both works explore the theme of the fragility of humanity and civilization and the unguarded impetus towards chaos inherent in us all.<br><br>Or it’s a fun book about a guy stuck on an island with beast people.<br><br>The character of Dr. Moreau himself can be seen as an extension of Dr. Frankenstein, willfully toying with the mysteries of creation for his own scientific curiosity and blithely uncaring about his experiments until he is forced to deal with it. In this sense, Wells’ work is fundamentally tied to modern writing about the morality and ethics of genetics and with the integrity of our science and technology and how it affects nature.<br><br>Published a couple of years before Joseph Conrad’s brilliant <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4900.Heart_of_Darkness" title="Heart of Darkness by Joseph Conrad" rel="nofollow noopener">Heart of Darkness</a>, this also provokes thought about the intellectual climate of the end of the 1800s to lead such talented writers towards these questions.<br><br>Sometimes this can be painfully dated and the language is in that stilted Victorian prose, and there are some gaps in the plot, but this is a seminal work that should be read for fans of speculative fiction.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1573596688i/28441714.jpg" width="400" height="248" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_18619525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18619525" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18619525?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375760969" href="/book/show/573178.The_Island_of_Dr_Moreau">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">SciFi and/or Horror Geeks</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=mieville50">mieville50</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18619525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17675733936370237593">Much creepier than I expected and much smarter, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29981.The_Island_of_Dr__Moreau' title='The Island of Dr. Moreau by H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Island of Dr. Moreau</a>, as with so much of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/880695.H_G__Wells' title='H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>H.G. Wells</a>' science fiction, addressed the ethical pitfalls of a scientific eventuality far too early to be anything other than prophetic, yet it still manages to be more entertaining than preachy. <br/><br/>Edward Prendick finds himself shipwrecked on an island with Doctors Montgomery and Moreau. The former a follower of the latter, who just happens to be a mad vivisectionist. Beyond these scientists, Prendick fi</span>
  <span id="freeText17675733936370237593" style="display:none">Much creepier than I expected and much smarter, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29981.The_Island_of_Dr__Moreau" title="The Island of Dr. Moreau by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Island of Dr. Moreau</a>, as with so much of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/880695.H_G__Wells" title="H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">H.G. Wells</a>' science fiction, addressed the ethical pitfalls of a scientific eventuality far too early to be anything other than prophetic, yet it still manages to be more entertaining than preachy. <br /><br />Edward Prendick finds himself shipwrecked on an island with Doctors Montgomery and Moreau. The former a follower of the latter, who just happens to be a mad vivisectionist. Beyond these scientists, Prendick finds himself intensely weirded out by the other inhabitants of the island, frightening man-animals created by Dr. Moreau. <br /><br />Moreau captures the island's animals and painfully turns them into half-men, then forces them to live by strict standards that he believes will overcome their bestial natures. Moreau's primary commandment is that they cannot eat meat. This is, of course, a recipe for suspense and horror, for how can one expect Leopard Men or Puma Men to curb their need for meat, when the humans conducting the experiments cannot curb their own bestial natures? It simply can't be done.<br /><br />Prendick finds himself becoming a participant, although not entirely willingly, in Moreau's society of vivisection. And once the animals finally rebel, as we know they must, he becomes the last man on the island, watching the tortured animals return to their natures and throw off Moreau's pseudo-society.<br /><br />Even now, one hundred and thirteen years after it was written, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29981.The_Island_of_Dr__Moreau" title="The Island of Dr. Moreau by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Island of Dr. Moreau</a> is spooky enough to work as an effective horror/sci-fi story, but its still relevant thematic depth is what makes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29981.The_Island_of_Dr__Moreau" title="The Island of Dr. Moreau by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">Moreau</a> essential to anyone who loves books. Genetics (eugenics), animal experimentation, psychology, colonization, imperialism, patriarchy, scientific chauvinism, religion, and ethical imposition are seriously and intelligently explored. Wells' implied conclusions may be unsettling at times, but <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29981.The_Island_of_Dr__Moreau" title="The Island of Dr. Moreau by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Island of Dr. Moreau</a> will make you think. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Miéville" rel="nofollow noopener">China Mieville</a> says that Moreau is "a kind of fantasy echo of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">Shakespeare</a>’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12985.The_Tempest" title="The Tempest by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">The Tempest</a>." Could there be higher praise than that?</span>
  <a data-text-id="17675733936370237593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1529013372" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1529013372" />
    <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya"><img alt="Leonard Gaya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442984307p2/27098959.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1529013372?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leonard Gaya" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya">Leonard Gaya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27098959-leonard-gaya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1529013372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12007107852372050941">This book stems from an idea that is at the same time thought-provoking, insane and very tangible. That is probably the reason why it is so scary. It is a classic of the victorian era, but for some reason probably not as famous as many other fictions of the “gothic” movement and indeed not as well known as a few other novels by H.G. Wells (such as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine' title='The Time Machine by H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Time Machine</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17184.The_Invisible_Man' title='The Invisible Man by H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Invisible Man</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8909.The_War_of_the_Worlds' title='The War of the Worlds by H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>The War of the Worlds</a>). But it definitely deserves to be read again today.<br/><br/>The plot is rather simple: a castaway</span>
  <span id="freeText12007107852372050941" style="display:none">This book stems from an idea that is at the same time thought-provoking, insane and very tangible. That is probably the reason why it is so scary. It is a classic of the victorian era, but for some reason probably not as famous as many other fictions of the “gothic” movement and indeed not as well known as a few other novels by H.G. Wells (such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Machine</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17184.The_Invisible_Man" title="The Invisible Man by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Invisible Man</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8909.The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The War of the Worlds</a>). But it definitely deserves to be read again today.<br /><br />The plot is rather simple: a castaway by the name of Prendick ends up on an uncharted island in the Pacific Ocean and meets the infamous Dr. Moreau and his assistant. Dr. Moreau is known for having practiced vivisection experiments some years before in London and, as a result, was excluded from the scientific community. Prendick later discovers that Moreau has been carrying on with his experiments and has created some monstrous beasts, while trying to turn animals into some wretched semblance of human beings. This discovery is planted and built up with some amount of suspense right from the first pages until it is fully exposed around the middle of the novel, in the chapter entitled “<i>Doctor Moreau Explains</i>”. The second part of the book is a nail-biting account of the catastrophic series of events that follow the dreadful discovery.<br /><br />Obviously, “The Island of Dr. Moreau” is in the same vein as Gustav Meyrink’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99794.The_Golem" title="The Golem by Gustav Meyrink" rel="nofollow noopener">The Golem</a> or Shelley’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">Frankenstein</a> (it can also evoke earlier figures like Shakespeare’s Prospero and Caliban in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12985.The_Tempest" title="The Tempest by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">The Tempest</a>, or even Shylock’s most famous lines in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24128.The_Merchant_of_Venice" title="The Merchant of Venice by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">The Merchant of Venice</a>). It is about the <i>hubris</i> of men attempting to imitate God and create a human being with the help of science. The result is invariably dreadful and deadly. Wells original treatment of this theme rests upon the idea of vivisectional experiments carried on animals in an isolate place: mammals are surgically and chemically modified to look and behave as much as humans as they possibly can. But the mental distress caused by this novel lies of the fact that these attempts are always cruel, disastrous and abortive. The Isserley of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/123063.Under_the_Skin" title="Under the Skin by Michel Faber" rel="nofollow noopener">Under the Skin</a> is a distant relative of Moreau's creatures.<br /><br />In some way, this book presages WWII’s Nazi’s “scientific” testings or even today's plausible out-of-line genetic engineering initiatives. Wells closes the book with these words: “<i>the manufacture of monsters - an perhaps even quasi-human monsters - is within the possibilities of vivisection.</i>” But, more deeply, perhaps, the horror lies in the fact that this fiction shows how feeble and unreal our human values are (including religious ones), and how easily men can fall back below animality.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12007107852372050941" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1342413" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1342413" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1342413?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375760969" href="/book/show/573178.The_Island_of_Dr_Moreau">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=19th-c-brit">19th-c-brit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1342413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10195880096755947626"><br/>Popular historian and utopian novelist H.G. Wells is sometimes thought of as the “anti-Gibbon”: whereas Edward Gibbon devoted himself to studying a culture’s “decline and fall”, H.G. Well’s celebrates the march of progress, showing how our culture, despite many obvious setbacks, moves on toward greater and greater achievements. But Wells, although an optimist by nature, was also a gifted literary artist, and when he seized upon an idea with disquieting implications, he did not hesitate to explor</span>
  <span id="freeText10195880096755947626" style="display:none"><br />Popular historian and utopian novelist H.G. Wells is sometimes thought of as the “anti-Gibbon”: whereas Edward Gibbon devoted himself to studying a culture’s “decline and fall”, H.G. Well’s celebrates the march of progress, showing how our culture, despite many obvious setbacks, moves on toward greater and greater achievements. But Wells, although an optimist by nature, was also a gifted literary artist, and when he seized upon an idea with disquieting implications, he did not hesitate to explore them. <i>The Island of Dr. Moreau</i>, perhaps the greatest and most disturbing of his “scientific romances,” is an example of his uncompromising art at its best.<br /><br />The plot is straightforward. The shipwrecked Edward Prendick ends up on an island presided over by the once notorious but now discredited surgeon Dr. Moreau, who has dedicated his life to transforming animals into humans by a series of painful operations. His more successful failures (all his works are failures) have formed a society on the other side of the island, where—with the doctor’s help--they have created an ethical system that “men” like them should follow, and a religion too, in which above all else Dr. Moreau and his laboratory (the House of Pain) are both reverenced and feared.<br /><br />The book has many themes, the most obvious of which are the morality of both animal experimentation (or “vivisection,” as it was called in Well’s time) and the use of pain in experimentation, but also touches upon the twin processes of evolution and degeneration, the nature of religion, the character of a man who would play God, and—yes—even the character of God himself and the deplorable semi-human beings that he “creates.” This last theme is perhaps the reason why an older Wells once referred to this book as “an exercise in youthful blasphemy.”<br /><br />To give you an idea, here is a bit of the most blasphemous portion of the book, in which Dr. Moreau explains himself to Prendick:<blockquote>
  <i>“So for twenty years altogether — counting nine years in England — I have been going on; and there is still something in everything I do that defeats me, makes me dissatisfied, challenges me to further effort. Sometimes I rise above my level, sometimes I fall below it; but always I fall short of the things I dream...These creatures of mine seemed strange and uncanny to you so soon as you began to observe them; but to me, just after I make them, they seem to be indisputably human beings. It’s afterwards, as I observe them, that the persuasion fades. First one animal trait, then another, creeps to the surface and stares out at me. But I will conquer yet! Each time I dip a living creature into the bath of burning pain, I say, ‘This time I will burn out all the animal; this time I will make a rational creature of my own!...They go. I turn them out when I begin to feel the beast in them, and presently they wander there. They all dread this house and me. There is a kind of travesty of humanity over there...There’s something they call the Law. Sing hymns about ‘all thine.’ They build themselves their dens, gather fruit, and pull herbs — marry even. But I can see through it all, see into their very souls, and see there nothing but the souls of beasts, beasts that perish, anger and the lusts to live and gratify themselves. — Yet they’re odd; complex, like everything else alive. There is a kind of upward striving in them, part vanity, part waste sexual emotion, part waste curiosity. It only mocks me….And now,” said he, standing up after a long gap of silence, during which we had each pursued our own thoughts, “what do you think? Are you in fear of me still?”</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="10195880096755947626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13952976469219724993">3 to 3.5 stars<br/><br/>A quick classic! Good, but not great.<br/><br/>It feels like this was Wells’ treatise on science playing God thinly veiled in a story. It is about 2/3 textbook dissertation about the possibilities and ramifications of body modification/species merge. The other 1/3 is the actual story of action and mayhem on Dr. Moreau’s island.   <span id="freeText13952976469219724993" style="display:none">3 to 3.5 stars<br /><br />A quick classic! Good, but not great.<br /><br />It feels like this was Wells’ treatise on science playing God thinly veiled in a story. It is about 2/3 textbook dissertation about the possibilities and ramifications of body modification/species merge. The other 1/3 is the actual story of action and mayhem on Dr. Moreau’s island. Many times this story made me think about Frankenstein. I mean, are there any stories of mad scientist creators where their insane experiments that throw caution to the wind end well?<br /><br />If you want to complete your list of sci-fi classics, be sure to check this out. If you are hoping for some super-creepy, scary, blood-chillingly dark adventures, I am thinking you might be a bit let down. Not for everyone, but if you like speculative fiction based on weird science, this will be right up your alley!<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2586201336" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2586201336" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2586201336?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780575095168" href="/book/show/7895286-the-island-of-dr-moreau">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2586201336" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5419568341911626042"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1541556157i/26557326._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>“Before, they had been beasts, their instincts fitly adapted to their surroundings, and happy as living things may be. Now they stumbled in the shackles of humanity, lived in a fear that never died, fretted by a law they could not understand; their mock-human existence, begun in an agony, was one long internal struggle, one long dread of Moreau.” - H.G. Wells, <i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> <br/><br/><i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> is H.G. Wells’ 1896 classic tale of a mad scientist creating nearly two hundred h</span>
  <span id="freeText5419568341911626042" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1541556157i/26557326._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>“Before, they had been beasts, their instincts fitly adapted to their surroundings, and happy as living things may be. Now they stumbled in the shackles of humanity, lived in a fear that never died, fretted by a law they could not understand; their mock-human existence, begun in an agony, was one long internal struggle, one long dread of Moreau.” - H.G. Wells, <i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> <br><br><i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> is H.G. Wells’ 1896 classic tale of a mad scientist creating nearly two hundred hybrid beings resembling humans by way of vivisection on animals, a work judged by critics at the time as too blasphemous and too disturbing to warrant publication. Hey, why not take such harsh reaction as a great reason to read this short novel sooner rather than later.<br><br>Let me tell you folks, <i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> is one humdinger of an adventure story to keep you on the edge of your seat from the first page to last, with elements of Frankenstein, The Fugitive, Lost and Survivor. The entire novel is a written account of events as recorded by Edward Prendick, an Englishman educated in biology at university. Young Prendick survives days on a dingy following a shipwreck and is picked up by another ship scheduled to make a first stop at an obscure Pacific island. While onboard, Prendick is brought back to health by a passenger with a background in medicine, a man by the name of Montgomery.<br><br>Turns out this gruff, one-time Londoner is joined by his strange, bestial servant, M'ling. And Montgomery also has a host of animals aboard. The frequently drunk Captain doesn't like the grotesque M'ling or the animals on his ship and lashes out at Montgomery. Prendick tells the Captain to "shut up" - a huge mistake he confesses in retrospect. When they near the island, the Captain forces Prendick off his ship and back on his dingy. Montgomery takes pity on the naturalist and brings him along to his island. Prendick eventually meets Doctor Moreau and becomes, by degrees, more aware of the many horrifying experiments conducted over the course of years in island isolation.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1541296616i/26544104._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And many are the questions raised by those experiments and the underlying methods and ideas concocted by Doctor Moreau. The most obvious question pertains to the very act of dissecting live animals for the purposes of experimentation. Nowadays, of course, we oppose such practice but back when the novel was written vivisection was still a hotly debated topic. However, we still debate related biological issues such as gene splicing which is a specific example of the longstanding concerns hovering around the dangers of science.<br><br>Prendick’s interactions with such diverse creatures as Leopard Man, Saint Bernard Dog Man, Ape Man, Swine Woman, Silvery Hairy Man and a Bear-Bull cry out for our reflection on the differences between savagery and humanity, nature and civilization, order and chaos, freedom and control. And what about Doctor Moreau's explanation on how the experience of pain, a characteristic of our animal nature, has held humans back in their development, how, in order to become less animal and more fully human, pain must be transcended? Recall the popularity in England in the late nineteenth century of the philosophy of utilitarianism as articulated by such thinkers as John Stewart Mill, a philosophy placing a premium on seeking pleasure and avoiding pain.<br><br>Charles Darwin's theory of evolution was very much in the public mind and H.G. Wells certainly took Darwin seriously. Among other aspects, <i>The Island of Doctor Moreau</i> is aligned with Darwinian theory respecting how humans are different not in kind from animals but only in degree.<br><br>In keeping with the animal nature in man, H.G. Wells forces Edward Prendick to deal with those base qualities even before stepping foot on Doctor Moreau’s island. There’s the crisis in the dingy where Prendick and two other men are dying of thirst and hunger. The drawing of lots is proposed to determine who will die so two may live. Prendick refuses to participate, brandishing a knife to ward off attack. The other two men draw lots and when the stronger seaman loses he refuses to abide by the rules. The two grapple and tumble overboard to their death. A second foreshadow: that drunken captain declares himself the law and master ruling over all on his ship. If he says Prendick is to leave his ship then Prendick will leave his ship, even if it means the certain death of the young man – no question of humanity, decency or ethics comes into play.<br><br>Control of the Beast Men on the island centers around Pavlov-style conditioned reflex reinforcement. Obey the law and act more like humans or it is back to the House of Pain, that is, Doctor Moreau's operating table. Also added into the mix to enforce control and human-like behavior is chant and prayer. One can imagine the reaction to the novel from pious nineteenth century religious folk. In order to assert his own control and order, at one point Prendick even appeals to the existence of Moreau's second body in the sky looking down on the Beast Men once the doctor's physical body is dead. The philosophical dimensions of the tale go on and on and on.<br><br>Fast-paced adventure and a slew of lively probing questions along the way. There are many excellent reasons why this classic work is included as part of SF Masterworks.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1541556326i/26557330.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>H.G. Wells, 1866-1946</span>
  <a data-text-id="5419568341911626042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2860982769" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2860982769" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2860982769?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141441023" href="/book/show/341272.The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2860982769" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15008864512217466265"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood' title='Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>Margaret Atwood</a> reminds us in her introduction here of just how beloved <i>The Island Of Dr. Moreau</i> was by the inimitable <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges' title='Jorge Luis Borges' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jorge Luis Borges</a>, who called it an “atrocious miracle.” “Speaking of Wells’s early tales—<i>The Island Of Dr. Moreau</i> among them—he said ‘I think they will be incorporated, like the fables of Theseus or Ahasuerus, into the general memory of the species and even transcend the fame of their creator or the extinction of the language in which they were written.’” Just for the record, t</span>
  <span id="freeText15008864512217466265" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">Margaret Atwood</a> reminds us in her introduction here of just how beloved <i>The Island Of Dr. Moreau</i> was by the inimitable <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Jorge Luis Borges</a>, who called it an “atrocious miracle.” “Speaking of Wells’s early tales—<i>The Island Of Dr. Moreau</i> among them—he said ‘I think they will be incorporated, like the fables of Theseus or Ahasuerus, into the general memory of the species and even transcend the fame of their creator or the extinction of the language in which they were written.’” Just for the record, the last time I checked there were 370 million native English speakers, and another 1.7 billion who use it as a lingua franca. I’ll say this much for the novella, the onset of suspense is far subtler than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35031085.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">Frankenstein</a>, which I recently reread. <i>Frankenstein</i> moves relatively quickly to a kind of full-bore hysteria and remains there for the duration. Here, the building of suspense is more gradual and then it modulates as the danger falls and rises once more. Commendable too is the novella’s compressed action and vivid description. ”Colour vanished from the world, the tree tops rose against the luminous blue sky in inky silhouette.” Joseph Conrad’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4900.Heart_of_Darkness" title="Heart of Darkness by Joseph Conrad" rel="nofollow noopener">Heart of Darkness</a> seems the only peer I might cite. And like both Heart of Darkness and Frankenstein it is a Chinese-box narrative—a story within a story. The novella must be read as a product of its time, like Frankenstein. The reprehensible science here is vivisection between species. It’s a given of the narrative that such exchanges of tissue can occur. So swallow the Kool-Aid, and allow Wells his conceit. Frankenstein was about vivisection, too, and it first appeared in 1818. This novella was published in June 1895, which tells us something about how long these erroneous ideas of monster creation were popular with the general public. (Now we’ve got CRISPR and scads of far, far more horrifying technologies. The Chinese, it’s reported, are creating supermen, and so on.) <i>Dr. Moreau</i> is a part of our SF heritage. The prose is on the whole quite wonderful. The end is heartbreakingly sad and moving, especially if one loves animals and nature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15008864512217466265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1389616173" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1389616173" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1389616173?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=pre-80s-sf">pre-80s-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1389616173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12165134809090900937"><i>“Not to go on all-fours; that is the Law. Are we not Men?"<br/>“Not to suck up Drink; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br/>“Not to eat Fish or Flesh; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br/>“Not to claw the Bark of Trees; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br/>“Not to chase other Men; that is the Law. Are we not Men?”<br/>"You gotta fight for your right to paaaaarty!"</i><br/><br/>Sorry for a spoiler so early on but yes, The Beastie Boys are to be found on the unnamed – but titular – Island of Dr. Moreau. Sort of.<br/><br/>Interestingly <i>The Island </i></span>
  <span id="freeText12165134809090900937" style="display:none"><i>“Not to go on all-fours; that is the Law. Are we not Men?"<br>“Not to suck up Drink; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br>“Not to eat Fish or Flesh; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br>“Not to claw the Bark of Trees; that is the Law. Are we not Men?<br>“Not to chase other Men; that is the Law. Are we not Men?”<br>"You gotta fight for your right to paaaaarty!"</i><br><br>Sorry for a spoiler so early on but yes, The Beastie Boys are to be found on the unnamed – but titular – Island of Dr. Moreau. Sort of.<br><br>Interestingly <i>The Island of Dr. Moreau</i> begins with a brief introduction by "Charles Edward Prendick" – nephew of the novel’s protagonist Edward Prendick – explaining the background of his uncle’s first hand account of his shipwreck and the uncle's records of the time he spent on this mysterious island. From then on the first person narrative is switched to the original Edward Prendick and Charles is never heard from again. So basically what we have here is a precursor to the “found footage” trope often used in modern movies like The Blair Witch Project, Paranormal Activity etc. As a literary device this it seems to have gone out of fashion, nowadays we even get first person narratives written in the present tense which does not make sense when you think about it, but makes perfect sense if you just accept it as the author’s chosen narrative technique.<br><br>Basically, Edward Prendick is shipwrecked, rescued by an associate of Dr. Moreau and taken to a mysterious island where he finds a bunch of man-beasts living there. How did they come to be? Well, there is a scientist living there, the creatures worship him, and he is a few sandwiches short of a picnic, so all that should be fairly indicative! Prendick spends about a year on this island until one day <a href="http://www.urbandictionary.com/define.php?term=fit+hits+the+shan" rel="nofollow noopener">“the fit hits the shan”</a>. That is all the synopsis you need I think.<br><br>This book is what I would call “sci-fi horror”, it is probably Wells’ most horrific book, with all the vivisections, gore, and violence. In my previous reviews of H.G. Wells’ books I mentioned that he pioneered at least three standard sci-fi tropes: time traveling, alien invasion and genetic engineering. That last one is a reference to <i>The Island of Dr. Moreau</i> of course, but back then I have not reread this book yet and I forgot an important detail. There is no genetic engineering in this book! Animals are turned into imitations of men by Dr. Moreau through the process of vivisection, chemical injections, blood transfusion and brain surgery. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1467168562i/19559991.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>It is a little like cutting and pasting in word processing I think. If “Frankensteining” was a verb you can probably apply it to mad Moreau’s process. I find the science in this book is less convincing than Wells’ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8909.The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener"> The War of The Worlds</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Time Machine</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17184.The_Invisible_Man" title="The Invisible Man by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Invisible Man</a>. It is hard to believe Moreau is able to create intelligence or sentience through brain surgery and his technique of replacing and grafting limbs to animals seems more likely to make a mess than an anthropomorphized creature able to run around. The pain he depicts is convincing and disturbing, though.<br><br>Characterization is a little thin in this book, the human characters are fairly two dimensional, even Doc Moreau is your standard issue cranky and obsessive mad scientist. That said some of the “manimals” are quite adorable, like the dog-man who is loyal to Prendick to the last and a little sloth-like creature that Wells describes as repulsive but still sounds pretty cute to me. Wells is clearly against men playing God, slicing and dicing animals just to see what happen. All in the name of science and progress, of course, morals can get in the backseat and keep quiet. This short novel is very fast-paced; if you are inattentive for a few seconds you are likely to miss some important plot points. That should not be much of an issue though as there is never a dull moment in the book.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1467168562i/19559992.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The Beastie Boys fighting for their right.<br><br><i>The Island of Dr. Moreau</i> is well worth a read, of course, H.G. Wells always is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12165134809090900937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2235588033" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2235588033" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2235588033?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375760969" href="/book/show/573178.The_Island_of_Dr_Moreau">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=disturbing">disturbing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2235588033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9372477274171844144"><br/>I’ve decided to catch up some classics this year and H.G.Wells with <i> The Island of Dr Moreau</i> was my first choice. Rather successful I think. It worked for me on different levels. Less as horror story obviously more as a trigger to ponder about some valid and timeless questions. <br/><br/>How far can we go to satisfy our curiosity, do scientists to achieve their goal should be deaf to suffering of the objects of their studies, can we throw all the compassion and empathy and ethic and morals and scrupules o</span>
  <span id="freeText9372477274171844144" style="display:none"><br />I’ve decided to catch up some classics this year and H.G.Wells with <i> The Island of Dr Moreau</i> was my first choice. Rather successful I think. It worked for me on different levels. Less as horror story obviously more as a trigger to ponder about some valid and timeless questions. <br /><br />How far can we go to satisfy our curiosity, do scientists to achieve their goal should be deaf to suffering of the objects of their studies, can we throw all the compassion and empathy and ethic and morals and scrupules out the window? And aren't we responsible for effects of our experiments?<br /><br />For Wells a man studying nature becomes as merciless as Nature is itself. But in the end on the island it is pure instinct that defeats reason. Moreau is an extremely ambiguous figure, whether he is a demiurge, an omnipotent maker and creator bending the laws of nature to his caprices, mercilessly enforcing the law set for the human-like beings to keep them in obedience? Or is he an instrument of an absolute evolutionary process? He is not completely blind, he is aware that each of the created beings will sooner or later respond to the atavistic call proper to his inner nature and need. Who is he then?<br /><br />The novel was written in the last years of the nineteenth century and really humanity did not have to wait too long for terrible medical experiments, vivisection and other atrocities that make your flesh creep.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9372477274171844144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_719406380" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/719406380" />
    <a title="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5252054-irena"><img alt="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425497470p2/5252054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/719406380?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" href="/user/show/5252054-irena">ᴥ Irena ᴥ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook " href="/book/show/7675378-the-island-of-dr-moreau">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer719406380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14614448621024328185"><blockquote>
  <b>'What could it all mean? A locked enclosure on a lonely island, a notorious vivisector, and these crippled and distorted men?'</b>
</blockquote> This is the actual plot without any details. The details make this a very disturbing story. I forgot just how disturbing.<br/>It is interesting how this was an adventure when I first read it. Not a happy one, but still an adventure before anything else. Now, it is a horror story. <br/><br/>However you choose to see it, it will still be a horrifying account of Prendick's stay on the</span>
  <span id="freeText14614448621024328185" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>'What could it all mean? A locked enclosure on a lonely island, a notorious vivisector, and these crippled and distorted men?'</b>
</blockquote> This is the actual plot without any details. The details make this a very disturbing story. I forgot just how disturbing.<br />It is interesting how this was an adventure when I first read it. Not a happy one, but still an adventure before anything else. Now, it is a horror story. <br /><br />However you choose to see it, it will still be a horrifying account of Prendick's stay on the island. <br />The strongest and, of course, the most disturbing part of the story is Moreau's explanation of his work. The fact that he talks about it as if pain and suffering don't matter, makes it even worse. Combine that with the sounds of a tortured animal day after day and you'll get it. <blockquote>
  <b>'This time I will burn out all the animal.'</b>
</blockquote> I felt sorry for most of his subjects, but there is something simply disgusting about pigs and hyenas that sickened me every time they appeared. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14614448621024328185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_969436259" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/969436259" />
    <a title="Tom Quinn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31467977-tom-quinn"><img alt="Tom Quinn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574458226p2/31467977.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/969436259?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Quinn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Quinn" href="/user/show/31467977-tom-quinn">Tom Quinn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer969436259" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11167180573023132433">Something buried deep in my unconscious memory came alive and I barked, "Not to go on all fours, that is the law!" at my toddler, which reminded me it had been a long time since I read this sci-fi classic. <br/><br/>Revisited in 2020 and was impressed with how exciting and thought-provoking this is still today. Forgive it a slow beginning and a weak conclusion - this comes alive in a big way in the height of Act Two. 4 stars!</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
